 HDANALYSIS-U.S. consumers tiptoe along confidence cliff      BYBy Emily Kaiser      WC842 words   PD1 November 2007   ET16:25   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LPWASHINGTON, Nov 1 (Reuters) - The mighty U.S. consumer may be starting to crack, just as the Federal Reserve signaled that it was through with interest rate cuts barring a sharper economic downturn.   

Spending has held up remarkably well through months of nonstop news headlines about the free-falling housing market and record-high oil prices, propping up an economy that is limping through a housing recession.   
   TDBut consumer confidence has eroded, and that is beginning to show up in quiet retail stores and disappointing sales.   

Data released on Thursday showed personal spending rose at a modest 0.3 percent annual rate in September, down from a revised 0.5 percent in August and slightly below expectations. For details, see [ID:n01406094].   

"Over the next few months, as the latest spike in gas prices bites, we expect to see weaker spending on goods and discretionary services, so fourth-quarter consumption will be much slower than the 3 percent in the third quarter," said Ian Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High Frequency Economics.   

Retailers are already seeing signs of that.  Target Corp , which has recorded strong sales growth in recent years, lowered its sales forecast in September and October. Higher-end stores are starting to feel the pinch, too. Handbag maker  Coach Inc warned of weak traffic earlier this month, and forecast earnings below Wall Street expectations.   

BREACHING THE DAM   

Back in early 2001, Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan worried about an abrupt "break" in consumer confidence, and the central bank began a rate-cutting frenzy that eventually took the benchmark interest rate to 1 percent.   

In testimony to Congress, he likened the erosion in confidence to "water backing up against a dam that is finally breached. The torrent carries with it most remnants of certainty and euphoria that built up in earlier periods."   

Shortly after that testimony, the economy fell into recession, although it lasted all of eight months. The expansion that began in November 2001 appears to still be intact -- but confidence is once again showing deep cracks.   

The current Fed, under Chairman Ben Bernanke, lowered interest rates by a quarter point on Wednesday but sent a clear signal to Wall Street that unless economic conditions take a turn for the worse, further cuts should not be expected.   

There has not been a particularly strong link between consumer confidence and spending in recent years, but a swift decline in confidence often prompts people to retrench, and that is closely associated with recessions.   

The break that Greenspan spoke of in February 2001 first appeared in late November 2000, said Richard Curtin, director of the Reuters/University of Michigan Surveys of Consumers. The recession officially began four months later.   

In November 2000, the University of Michigan's consumer sentiment index stood at 107.6, according to Curtin's database. One month later, it was at 98.4, a 9 percent drop. Last week, the survey showed consumer sentiment at 80.9, the lowest in more than a year as concerns about the housing slump darkened the economic outlook.   

While the index is much lower now than it was when Greenspan testified in 2001, the key seems to be how quickly the mood changes. Curtin said a "break" occurs when there is a swift and widespread loss of confidence like the one in late 2000. While the October reading was poor, it was down only a modest 3 percent from the prior month.   

When asked what might trigger such a break this time, he said, "Housing -- that's the No. 1 candidate. And we're perilously close to $100-a-barrel oil. These two negative trends could join and create an overall downturn."   

COFFEE AND TOILET PAPER   

The downward trend is worrisome, however, and consumers are likely to be shouldering a heavier burden in the weeks to come as gasoline prices look set to rise.   

To make matters worse, food company Kraft said earlier this week that dairy prices were up about 40 percent.  Procter & Gamble , the largest U.S. consumer products maker, is raising prices on a host of everyday items including diapers, coffee and toilet paper.   

For now, a relatively strong job market and wage growth have many people feeling OK about their financial position. A recent Reuters/Zogby survey found that more than half of Americans polled rated their personal financial situation "good" or "excellent" -- yet only about a quarter gave U.S. economic policy such high marks.   

White House economic adviser Edward Lazear blames that on a "disconnect" between what people see in their own checkbooks and the bad news they read in the newspapers.   

"It's kind of a frustration because we can't quite understand the disconnect, but there definitely is one," he said at a recent round-table discussion.   

If consumers act on those negative attitudes this holiday season, the Fed may have to rethink its hawkish position.

 HDTop Stories in Asia Wednesday Oct 31 & the US Tuesday Oct 30      WC1,436 words   PD31 October 2007   ET08:53   SNMarket News International   SCMARNEW   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007. Market News International, Inc.   
   TD

*    [BOJ Stands Pat] The Bank of Japan on Wednesday said its 
nine-member policy board voted 8-1 to maintain the target for the 
overnight call rate at 0.5% in the face of easing but lingering 
uncertainties triggered by the US subprime mortgage crisis. The BOJ 
issued a brief statement on its "no change" decision at the end of a 
regular policy-setting meeting. Board member Atsushi Mizuno again voted 
against leaving short-term interest rates unchanged. 

*    [JGBs End Mixed] Japanese government bonds ended Wednesday's 
session narrowly mixed, with the longer end of the curve rallying 
modestly in the wake of the Bank of Japan's rate decision. Although the 
BOJ took the widely anticipated decision to leave monetary policy 
unchanged, ultra-long paper staged a modest relief rally. Continued 
month-end duration weighted demand also helped boost the longer dated 
paper. However, traders said volumes remained modest, with many players 
awaiting the outcome of the Federal Reserve's rate decision. Traders 
also noted that the upside appeared capped ahead of the 10-year auction 
due Thursday. The coupon was likely to be set at 1.60% - the lowest 
level since the BOJ ended its ultra-easy monetary policy in March 2006. 
The benchmark 10-year yield was 1 bp lower at 1.610%, while the lead 
Sept JGB futures contract was down 0.05 at 135.99

*    [Japan Stocks Reverse Course To End Higher] Japanese stock indices 
ended the day higher, as an afternoon rally helped push them into 
positive territory. The main indices ended the morning session lower, 
before rallying in the wake of the BOJ's decision to leave rates 
unchanged. The benchmark Nikkei 225 was higher by 86.62 points, or 
0.52%, at 16737.63. The broader-based TOPIX was 12.88 points higher at 
1620.07.

*    [Euro Hits Fresh Lifetime High] Major forex pairs were tied to 
narrow ranges in Asian trade Weds, as the market awaited the FOMC rate 
decision. However, euro-dollar did manage to squeeze out a fresh 
lifetime high at $1.4447, although an option barrier ahead of $1.4450 
again capped the upside. The pair spent the entire Asian session stuck 
in a $1.4434/47 range. Dollar-yen similarly declined, as the BOJ 
took the widely expected decision to leave monetary policy unchanged. 
The pair was unable to break out of a Y114.62/81 range, with offers 
layered ahead of Y115.00 capping the upside. 

*    [BOJ: Japan Economy To Show Sustained Growth] The Bank of Japan on 
Wednesday said the economy will show sustained growth of "around 2% on 
average," or just above its potential rate, in the next 18 months and 
inflation should recover slowly from zero, with the core CPI seen 
"around zero" in fiscal 2007 and "around 0.5%" in the following year. 
The central bank also said in its semi-annual Outlook that "weak 
inflationary pressures have given the bank latitude in conducting 
monetary policy." 

*    [Japan Average Earnings Lower In Sept] The average monthly cash 
earnings per regular employee in Japan, including overtime and bonuses, 
fell 0.5% from a year earlier in September to Y273,144 ($2,381), the 
first drop in two months, the Ministry of Health, Labor and Welfare said 
Wednesday. It followed a revised 0.6% gain in August. 

*    [Japan Construction Orders Slump In Sept] The value of construction 
orders received in September by the 50 largest contractors in Japan fell 
16.3% year-on-year to Y1.57 trillion, down for the third consecutive 
month, the Ministry of Land, Infrastructure and Transport said 
Wednesday. The September fall followed a 14.2% decline in August and a 
10.4% drop in July and remained the largest decline since the 20.1% fall 
recorded in July 2006. 

*    [Aussie Private Sector Credit Up In Sept] Credit provided to the 
private sector by Australian financial intermediaries rose 1.2% in 
September from August and increased 15.9% from a year earlier, the 
Reserve Bank of Australia (RBA) said. In August, private sector credit 
increased by a revised 1.4% from July. The initial estimate was that it 
had increased 1.5%, the RBA said.

*    [Aussie Building Approvals Up In Sept] Building approvals in 
Australia rose a seasonally adjusted 6.8% to 13,710 in September from 
August and were up 4.2% from a year earlier, the Australian Bureau of 
Statistics (ABS) said. The market consensus was for a 1.0% rise 
month-on-month.

[In The US]
*    U.S. October Conference Board consumer confidence measure Tuesday 
slipped to 95.6 vs. a 99.5 revised September reading as it continues to 
hover at two-year lows and portends a slower consumer. The present 
situation index eased to 118.8 from 121.2, while expectations fell to 
80.1 in October from 85.0. Plans to buy autos edged up, but plans to buy 
appliance and homes are down. One-year inflation expectations are 5.1% 
vs. 5.0% in September. 

*    Pessimists now outnumber optimists in the assessment of future 
business conditions, a key reading from the Conference Board's consumer 
confidence report that survey chief Lynn Franco warns is pointing 
squarely at slowing economic growth through the fourth quarter. "For the 
first time in quite some time we see that the pessimists, those that are 
telling us business conditions are worse, are at 13.8% and slightly 
outnumber the optimists at only 13.7% who are saying business conditions 
are getting better," Franco said in a phone interview Tuesday. 

*    The August S&P/Case-Shiller Composite-20 U.S. home price index 
slipped to 197.16, down 0.8% month-over-month and the text said this was 
the sharpest year-over-year drop since June 1991, with the decline led 
by Florida.

*    U.S. Senate Budget Committee Chairman Kent Conrad told Market 
News International Tuesday that he hopes a hearing his panel is holding 
Wednesday on long-term budget issues will "kick start" a serious debate 
on the nation's fiscal challenges. "We are heading for a cliff and we 
have to change direction. But nothing is happening. We are in a crisis 
but there is not a sense of urgency around here. We need a sense of 
urgency," Conrad said. Conrad's panel will hold a hearing Wednesday 
morning on a proposal he has drafted with Sen. Judd Gregg, the ranking 
Republican on the panel, to create a fiscal task force to assess 
long-term fiscal trends and offer recommendations about how to revamp 
spending and tax policies. 

*    U.S. House Majority Leader Steny Hoyer said Tuesday that 
congressional Democrats continue to work on fiscal year 2008 spending 
bills, but said President Bush has done little to help their efforts. 
"We need some cooperation from the president," Hoyer said. He said it is 
important to complete work on the FY'08 budget this fall before Congress 
adjourns for the year. Bush and Democratic leaders in Congress have been 
skirmishing for months on the overall level for discretionary spending 
which is distributed in the 12 spending bills. 

*    U.S. auto sales are expected to show little or no overall growth 
in October as the effects of a depressed housing market ripple through 
the economy, and high gasoline prices continue to cut into personal 
budgets, dealers and analysts said. But dealers in some areas reported 
sales were holding up and even registering double-digit gains as those 
local markets bucked the national trend. 

*    A near shutdown in originations, a dramatic fall in new issuance 
volumes, and a widening of spreads in CMBS have driven market spreads 
back towards 5-year highs -- all while underlying credit fundamentals 
remain rock solid. Based on September remittance reports just released 
last week, the overall CMBS delinquency rate has continued to fall with 
no sign that weakness in U.S. residential subprime is in anyway spilling 
over to commercial real estate (CRE) credit performance. 

*    On the eve of the Federal Reserve's interest rate decision, a top 
Treasury official Tuesday said that despite strong economic 
fundamentals, the decline in the U.S. housing sector remains "most 
significant current risk" to the economy. In remarks to the Women in 
Housing and Finance, Treasury Assistant Secretary for Financial 
Institutions David Nason noted the recent stress in the credit and 
mortgage markets, which he said is "occurring against the backdrop of a 
strong global economy," as well as solid U.S. economic fundamentals. "In 
our view, the underlying strength of the economy should enable further 
continued growth," Nason said.

 HDSummary Box:  Federal Reserve  cuts  interest rate  to help ease strain of housing slump      BYBy The Associated Press      WC118 words   PD1 November 2007   ET02:07   SNAssociated Press Newswires   SCAPRS   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007.    LPANOTHER CUT: The Federal Reserve sliced an important interest rate Wednesday -- its second reduction in the last six weeks -- to help the economy survive a deepening housing slump that is likely to crimp growth in coming months.   

HOW MUCH: Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke and all but one of his colleagues agreed to lower the federal funds rate by one-quarter percentage point to 4.50 percent.   
   TDIN RESPONSE: Commercial banks announced that they were cutting their prime lending rate -- for certain credit cards, home equity lines of credit and other loans -- by a corresponding amount, to 7.50 percent.

 HDBank of America cut key  interest rate  after  Federal Reserve  decision      WC135 words   PD31 October 2007   ET19:27   SNAssociated Press Newswires   SCAPRS   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007.  The Associated Press.  All Rights Reserved.   
   LPNEW YORK (AP) -  Bank of America on Wednesday lowered its prime rate by one-quarter of a percentage point after the Federal Reserve cut its key interest rate.   

Bank of America said its prime rate is now 7.50 percent, down from 7.75 percent. The Fed cut the federal funds rate, which is what banks charge each other for overnight loans, to 4.50 percent from 4.75 percent previously.   
   TDThe prime rate is what banks charge their best customers, and serves as the benchmark for lending rates to all customers for mortgages, credit cards and other products.   

Most banks typically raise or lower their prime rate by an amount equal to the Fed's rate action within minutes of its decision.

 HDShares of national banks fall on heels of  interest rate  cut by the  Federal Reserve    WC231 words   PD31 October 2007   ET19:50   SNAssociated Press Newswires   SCAPRS   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007.  The Associated Press.  All Rights Reserved.   
   LPNEW YORK (AP) - Shares of national banks fell after the Federal Reserve cut a key interest rate to 4.5 percent from 4.75 percent Wednesday afternoon.   

Banks have been struggling in recent months due to the deterioration in the housing and credit markets. Credit markets have tightened as borrowers have gone increasingly delinquent and into default on loans, especially subprime mortgages given to customers with poor credit history.   
   TDThe key rate, called the federal funds rate is the rate banks charge each other. It is often used to calculate interest rates on typical consumer and business loans. The cut will likely be reflected in banks' prime lending rate, making borrowing more attractive for individuals and businesses. The prime rate was at 7.75 percent and will likely fall to 7.5 percent in the coming days.   

Here's how the nation's largest banks performed following the Fed's decision:   

Shares of  Bank of America Inc. fell 59 cents to $47.40.   

Wells Fargo & Co. shares fell 56 cents to $33.44.   

Washington Mutual shares fell 26 cents to $27.85.   

Shares of  Citigroup Inc. fell 47 cents to $41.64   

JPMorgan Chase & Co. added 5 cents and is trading at $46.61.   

Wachovia Corp. shares fell 78 cents to $45.19.

 HDFed injects $41 billion into US financial system to help ease credit problems      BYBy JEANNINE AVERSA      CRAP Economics Writer   WC439 words   PD1 November 2007   ET19:37   SNAssociated Press Newswires   SCAPRS   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007.  The Associated Press.  All Rights Reserved.   
   LPWASHINGTON (AP) - The Federal Reserve pumped $41 billion (euro28.4 billion) into the U.S. financial system Thursday, the largest cash infusion since September 2001, to help companies get through a credit crunch.   

The action comes one day after Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke and all but one of his central bank colleagues voted to slice a key interest rate for the second time in six weeks to protect the economy from the ill effects of collapse in the housing market, aggravated by the credit troubles.   
   TDThe cash injection also came as Wall Street took a nosedive Thursday. The Dow Jones industrials were down more than 250 points in afternoon trading.   

The Fed on Wednesday ordered its key rate, called the federal funds rate, to be lowered by one-quarter percentage point to 4.50 percent. That followed up on a bolder, half-percentage point cut in September. Those two rate reductions might be sufficient to help the economy make its way safely through trouble spots, Fed policymakers indicated.   

The funds rate affects many other interest rates charged to millions of individuals and businesses and is the Fed's most potent tool for influencing economic activity.   

The  Federal Reserve Bank of New York, which carries out the central bank's open market operations, moved Thursday to inject $41 billion (euro28.4 billion) in temporary reserves into the U.S financial system. It came as part of ongoing efforts designed to ensure that the markets -- which have suffered through a period of turbulence over the last few months -- function smoothly. The cash infusion came in three separate operations.   

A New York Fed spokesman said it was the largest single day of operations since $50.35 billion was pumped into the system on Sept. 19, 2001, following the terror strikes on New York and Washington. He declined further comment.   

Fed policymakers at their meeting on Wednesday noted that the "strains from financial markets have eased somewhat on balance." Still many Fed officials in the last week have described the state of financial markets as fragile. Bernanke and other Fed officials have said it will take time for the markets to fully recover from the credit crisis.   

Since August, the Fed has been pumping cash into the financial system to help ease strains from the credit crunch. It also has cut its lending rate to banks -- a third such cut came on Wednesday. The Fed also has ordered two reductions to its most important interest rate, the funds rate, to help the situation.

 HDGold trades above $800 for 1st time since 1980 following  Federal Reserve 's  interest rate  cut      BYBy LAUREN VILLAGRAN      CRAP Business Writer   WC778 words   PD31 October 2007   ET21:04   SNAssociated Press Newswires   SCAPRS   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007.  The Associated Press.  All Rights Reserved.   
   LPNEW YORK (AP) - Gold barreled above $800 an ounce Wednesday for the first time since 1980 as investors cheered the Federal Reserve's decision to lower its benchmark interest rate by a quarter point.   

The Fed dropped its federal funds rate to 4.50 percent, as the markets widely anticipated. Lower interest rates tend to undermine the dollar and raise the allure of precious metals as an investment alternative. The dollar stumbled to another low against the euro following the Fed's decision on Wednesday, helping drive gold higher.   
   TDAlthough the regular trading sessions of most commodities markets were closed before the Fed released its decision, gold prices continued to climb in aftermarket trading. An ounce of gold gained $7.50 to settle at $795.30 an ounce on the New York Mercantile Exchange, then rose to a high of $800.80 ounce in later electronic activity.   

Gold last topped $800 an ounce in 1980, when prices reached as high as $875 an ounce in January. Adjusted for inflation, an $800 ounce of gold in 1980 would be worth more than $2,000 today.   

While the $800 mark means something less than it did 27 years ago, gold prices have surged roughly $150 an ounce since mid-August and that means consumers could see prices on gold jewelry go up ahead of the holiday season. But some jewelers say gold at $800 an ounce still isn't high enough to dent demand.   

"Last time it was the talk of the town," said Keith Hurdle, owner of Hurdle's Jewelry in Boulder, Colo., referring to gold's last foray above $800. "Not anymore, they don't even ask. If the piece is in their price range, they're fine."   

In its statement, the Fed said strains in the financial markets have eased somewhat since the summer's turmoil. But the central bank also cautioned that an intensifying housing slump could slow the pace of economic growth and warned that increasing energy and commodity prices raise the risks of inflation.   

The euro, which hit a high for the fourth straight trading day, bought a record over $1.45 in late trading on Wednesday.   

"Gold is a hedge against uncertainty, against currency volatility," said Carlos Sanchez, precious metals analyst with CPM Group. "It's a hedge against inflation. All this going on is supportive of higher gold prices."   

December silver jumped 11 cents to close at $14.438 an ounce on the Nymex.   

January platinum added $6.70 to end at $1,447.60 an ounce on the Nymex.   

Following the Fed's rate cut, energy prices extended a rally that was begun early in the session after the government posted a surprising draw on crude oil inventories for the second week in a row. Light, sweet crude for December delivery gained $4.15 to settle at $94.53 a barrel on the Nymex, after briefly hitting a record $94.74.   

Like gold, oil has approached the inflation-adjusted highs of early 1980. Depending on the calculation, a $38 barrel of crude then would fetch $96 to $101 or more today.   

The Energy Information Administration reported U.S. stockpiles of crude plunged by 3.9 million barrels in the week ended Oct. 26, compared with analysts' consensus forecast for a gain of 100,000 barrels. Expectations had ranged between a build of 2 million barrels and a draw of 2.6 million barrels.   

Gasoline inventories rose by 1.3 million barrels, while distillate inventories increased by 800,000 barrels. Analysts had expected moderate decreases of both.   

Gasoline futures for November rose 8.29 cents to $2.34 a gallon. November heating oil futures added 8.32 cents to $2.5078 a gallon. The November contracts for gas and heating oil expired at the session's end.   

Base metals ended mixed on the London Metal Exchange, with nickel, copper and lead finishing higher while zinc and aluminum prices slipped. Copper for December delivery on the Nymex slipped 0.85 cent to close at $3.473 a pound.   

Agriculture futures also ended mixed after a choppy week that has seen prices move in tandem with other markets, including equities.   

"We're down one day, up the next," said DTN analyst Gary Wilhelmi. "Outside markets are strong influences."   

Wheat for December delivery fell 6 cents to $8.08 a bushel on the Chicago Board of Trade. Soybeans for January picked up 16.5 cents to $10.2575 a bushel, while December corn rose 5.25 cents to $3.755 a bushel.

 HDU.S. Fed cuts rates again --- Quarter-point move signals central bank sees risks in balance      BYBy Greg Ip      WC660 words   PD1 November 2007   SNThe Wall Street Journal Asia   SCAWSJ   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here   http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns
   LPThe U.S. Federal Reserve cut its target for short-term interest rates a quarter of a percentage point but sought in its accompanying statement to dispel expectations of more rate cuts.   

As markets had widely expected, the Fed cut the target for the federal-funds rate, charged on overnight loans between banks, to 4.5% from 4.75%, on the heels of a half-point cut in September.   
   TDThe two moves together, the Fed said in its accompanying statement, "help forestall some of the adverse effects on the broader economy that might otherwise arise" from the summer credit crunch that drove up interest rates paid by many homeowners, corporations and banks.   

But the statement, in an apparent attempt to rein in market expectations of more cuts, said it now saw the risks of weaker growth and higher inflation as "roughly balanced."   

Stocks and bonds sold off on the news. The Dow Jones Industrial Average , up over 80 points before the Fed's afternoon announcement, initially fell into negative territory. Long-term bond prices, which move in the opposite direction of yields, fell. The statement appears to sharply reduce the odds the Fed will cut rates again at its December meeting, as markets had expected.   

The statement also reflected continuing concern about inflation. "Readings on core inflation have improved modestly this year, but recent increases in energy and commodity prices, among other factors, may put renewed upward pressure on inflation . . . Some inflation risks remain, and it will continue to monitor inflation developments carefully."   

For the first time this year, the vote by the 10 voting members of the Federal Open Market Committee (seven regional bank presidents participate but do not vote), was not unanimous. Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City President Thomas Hoenig dissented, saying he would have preferred no cut.   

Mreover, only six of the Fed's 12 reserve banks requested the parallel decrease in the less important discount rate, charged on direct Fed loans to banks, to 5% from 5.25%.   

Those signals suggest the decision to cut rates may have been close. It may have been driven in part by a desire not to disappoint markets. With futures markets assigning a 92% probability Wednesday morning to a cut, a failure to deliver would have been the biggest surprise in the 13 years for which comparable data are available, according to Bianco Research LLC, a Chicago financial-research firm. That would likely have led to sharp declines in stock prices and a possible resurgence of risk aversion in debt markets.   

But while avoiding that fallout may have helped tilt the Fed toward a cut, economic and strategic considerations were almost certainly more important. While the Fed expects the economy to emerge from its housing-induced slow period by the middle of next year, the risks around that forecast are of weaker, not stronger growth. Economists on average put the odds of recession in the next 12 months at 34%.   

Mr. Bernanke and other Fed officials say "risk management" -- a phrase coined by previous Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan -- requires them to base interest-rate decisions not just on the likeliest forecast, but on less likely but potentially far more damaging outcomes. Thus, a rate cut could be justified on the possibility that housing activity and prices fall even further than currently envisioned, causing more loss of wealth and more mortgage delinquencies and market turmoil. For the Fed, cutting now may avoid having to cut even more later. Moreover, the risks of such a move appear acceptable: Inflation, by the Fed's preferred measure, has been below 2%, the upper end of what most officials consider acceptable, and with unemployment rising from 4.4% in April to 4.7% in September (October's figure is released tomorrow).

 HDFED FOCUS-Dissent reveals growing hawkishness in Fed ranks      BYBy Alister Bull      WC564 words   PD31 October 2007   ET22:51   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LPWASHINGTON, Oct 31 (Reuters) - It wasn't an outright mutiny, but the split in the ranks of the Federal Reserve over the rate cut on Wednesday exposes concerns among a hawkish minority of policy-makers that tilt it against further easing.   

Kansas City Federal Reserve Bank President Thomas Hoenig dissented against a quarter-percentage point cut in the bellwether federal funds rate, preferring no change in rates, and only half of the nation's 12 regional Federal Reserve branches requested a matching reduction in the discount rate that governs direct Fed lending to banks.   
   TD"I don't think (Fed Chairman Ben) Bernanke is isolated, but you have a very strong minority that says, 'This is it'," said David Jones of Denver-based consultants DMJ Advisers.   

The Fed lowered the overnight federal funds rate to 4.5 percent as expected on Wedneday, but said recent increases in energy and commodity prices could renew pressure on inflation and risks of higher prices roughly balanced those of slower growth.   

"The hawks made their voices heard today," said Jones.   

It was Hoenig's fourth dissent since he took the reins in October 1991 of the Fed's tenth district, which includes Colorado, Kansas, Nebraska, Oklahoma, Wyoming, northern New Mexico and western Missouri.   

"Hoenig is a Fed veteran. It is hard to believe he would have dissented unless a few other Fed officials were on his side," wrote Roger Kubarych at  UniCredit.

"The implication is that bad housing figures, which are almost inevitable, won't be enough to compel the Fed to cut the funds rate (at the next meeting). Instead, it will take another sizable increase in the unemployment rate," he said.   

A government report on employment conditions in October, which will released on Friday, is expected to show 80,000 new jobs were created, while the unemployment rate is expected to remain unchanged at 4.7 percent.   

But a report from ADP Employer Services that U.S. private employers added 106,000 jobs in October was stronger than expected and hinted at a firmer overall labor market.   

In addition, the U.S. economy grew at a solid 3.9 percent annual rate in the third quarter, providing momentum to cushion activity in the final three months of the year as the economy deals with a housing slump and fallout from an August credit crunch.   

"While not yet 'Mutiny on the Bounty,' Tom Hoenig's vote against the additional quarter point cut is airing a growing minority view among central bankers that aggressive cutting of interest rates to rescue an ailing housing market may create even bigger inflation problems down the road,"  Wells Fargo senior economist Scott Anderson wrote in a note to clients.   

Indeed, interest rate futures scaled back the implied probability of another quarter percentage point cut at the Fed's next policy meeting on Dec. 11 to 42 percent from 64 percent overnight.   

That only six of the 12 regional Fed branches requested a quarter-point cut in the discount rate was "another indication that there was not widespread support for today's rate cut at the reserve banks," said Dean Maki, chief U.S. economist at  Barclays Capital.

 HDUPDATE 2-HK banks cut rates, currency retreats from ceiling      BYBy Susan Fenton and Donny Kwok      WC674 words   PD1 November 2007   ET05:25   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LPHONG KONG, Nov 1 (Reuters) - Hong Kong banks on Thursday said they would cut their prime lending rates in line with a reduction in U.S. borrowing costs, while the Hong Kong dollar eased away from the ceiling of its trading band with the U.S. dollar.   

The Hong Kong dollar has been bumping against the upper limit of its linked exchange rate system to the U.S. dollar, prompting the territory's central bank to intervene five times on Wednesday to keep its currency peg with the U.S. dollar intact.   
   TDThat intervention, which injected HK$7.8 billion (US$1 billion) into the banking system, pushed down interbank rates, giving banks leeway to cut prime rates on Thursday.   

The Hong Kong dollar is the second-most traded currency in Asia after the Japanese yen.   

The currency peg with the U.S. dollar forces the Hong Kong Monetary Authority (HKMA) to track U.S. interest rate moves but local banks have more flexibility in setting their prime rates, the rates they charge their most creditworthy borrowers.   

The central bank on Thursday lowered the base rate charged through its overnight discount window by 25 basis points to 6 percent after the U.S. Federal Reserve cut its benchmark federal funds rate by a quarter of a percentage point on Wednesday.   

HSBC Holdings Plc , the territory's biggest bank, its subsidiary Hang Seng Bank and other lenders, including Standard Chartered Bank ,  Bank of China (Hong Kong) and  Bank of East Asia , said they would reduce their prime rates from Friday by 25 basis points to between 7.25 and 7.5 percent.   

They will also cut their savings rates by a quarter point.   

The Hong Kong dollar <HKD=> is pegged at 7.8 to the U.S. dollar but can trade between 7.75 and 7.85. It fell on Thursday and was quoted at 7.7553 in late trade, after hitting its upper limit at 7.75 repeatedly on Wednesday, driven by strong demand for Hong Kong dollars to buy stocks.   

Analysts said there had also been signs of speculative trade.   

RUMOURS COOL   

Speculation dissipated on Thursday after the HKMA dismissed a report by U.S.-based research house Medley Global Advisors the previous day which said Hong Kong officials had lobbied Beijing to adjust the currency peg but Beijing had refused.   

The HKMA said the report was unfounded and its Chief Executive Joseph Yam said Hong Kong had no plans to re-peg the local dollar at a different level or widen its trading band. For details, double click on [ID:nHKG269070].   

"Maybe the speculation will take a rest for a bit," said Daniel Chan, senior investment strategist at DBS Bank.   

Economists say a de-pegging of the currency is unlikely.   

"We do not foresee a break in Hong Kong's currency link, because any move would be politically too sensitive for the (Hong Kong) government and, more importantly, for Beijing," investment bank  Credit Suisse said in a research note released on Thursday.   

Interbank rates have been trading at six-month highs amid a global credit squeeze that has made banks reluctant to lend to each other, and amid tight demand for Hong Kong dollars to fund new listings on the stock market.   

But the one-month interbank rate dropped to as low as 3.93/4.00 percent on Thursday, its lowest since February and down from 4.7 percent before the HKMA intervention on Wednesday.   

Analysts said interbank rates were dampened by both Wednesday's fund injection and the U.S. interest rate cut.   

Interbank rates are now back below U.S. dollar interbank rates and traders said carry trades, to take advantage of the interest rate differential by selling Hong Kong dollars for U.S. dollars, was re-emerging.   

(US$=HK$7.8) (Additional reporting by Christina Lo)

 HDANALYSIS-Are the world's central banks losing control?      BYBy Mike Dolan      WC1,022 words   PD31 October 2007   ET14:19   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LPLONDON, Oct 31 (Reuters) - Central banks, lauded as near infallible pilots of the monetary economy in recent years, are facing uncomfortable doubts about their collective grip on credit markets, interest rate structures and inflation.   

With the U.S. Federal Reserve expected to deliver its second official interest rate cut in less than six weeks on Wednesday -- less than three months after it declared inflation to be its predominant concern -- financial markets are growing sceptical.   
   TDAnd questions about how much sway the world's most influential central bank may be losing over its banking system are infecting its leading counterparts across the globe too.   

Since this year's slump in U.S. mortgage assets morphed into a full-blown global lending crisis in August, central banks' everywhere have struggled to retain traditionally lock-step control of short-term interbank lending rates critical to much of the borrowing in their economies.   

A private-sector tightening of credit conditions -- which saw bellwether three-month London interbank rates in euros, dollars, sterling and others jump more than half a percentage point regardless of central bank policy -- persists and has already crimped lending across the U.S. and Europe.   

Yet the Fed, faced with a steeper, housing-led slowdown than others, has raced to offset this with official rate cuts.   

And in doing so it has presided over a plummeting dollar and supercharged emerging markets, oil and commodity prices.   

The net result is that market inflation expectations -- crucial in assessing investor confidence in central banks keeping a lid on inflation over time -- are now rising. "The Fed's forecasting record is in tatters, the dollar is in tatters and inflation expectations are ticking up," said Nick Parsons, chief market strategist at nabCapital in London. "They are now in danger of losing control of every part of the yield curve from overnight out to 10-years."   

"Losing control of Libor may be unfortunate, but losing control of official rates is potentially disastrous."   

Whether he carries part of the blame or not, former Fed chief Alan Greenspan said this month he believes central banks, including the Fed, have struggled for years to influence long-term rates central to the pivotal housing boom and bust.   

"Central banks have essentially lost control of markets beyond 3, 4, 5 years out," Greenspan said.   

Fed communications, long its strength, are also in question.   

Speculation was rife this week that the Fed tried to orchestrate an 11th-hour dampening of expectations for another 25 basis point cut in its Fed funds target rate to 4.5 percent because markets had assigned a 100 percent probability to the move.   

This has fuelled a concern the Fed may be led into cutting rates simply because it fears disappointing housing and mortgage markets that are already depressing the economic outlook.   

The upshot is many now believe the Fed and other banks are willing tolerate higher inflation to square the circle.   

GREAT EXPECTATIONS   

Forecasting a rise in headline U.S. inflation to 3.4 percent in the fourth quarter from 2.8 percent now, strategists at Goldman Sachs think this price acceleration may well stick.   

Judging inflation expectations by bond market "breakevens" -- the difference between index-linked or inflation-protected government bond yields and those on underlying cash bonds -- Goldman sees a significant deterioration ahead.   

Ten-year breakevens are now 2.34 percent -- up more than a quarter of a point since the Fed started cutting rates and already well above the 1-2 percent long-term inflation levels most economists see the Fed being comfortable with.   

Goldman's Mike Vaknin sees these rising to 2.60 percent.   

"The Fed's easing policy...can have a detrimental effect on longer-term inflation expectations," Goldman said in a note to clients, adding soaring oil prices and a falling dollar would aggravate the price outlook over the coming months.   

Forced to suspend a planned interest rate rise in September, the European Central Bank's picture is not much better.   

Despite an uncontrolled tightening of lending conditions over the past two months -- where 3-month euro Libor is still more than half a percentage point above official rates -- the ECB is also seeing inflation and inflation expectations rising.   

Euro zone inflation surged to 2.6 percent in October from 2.1 percent the previous month, Eurostat reported on Wednesday. And ten-year breakevens in the euro area have risen to 2.3 percent from under 2 percent in August -- well above the ECB's target of 2 percent or just under it.   

And it's not just the big western central banks that have had monetary frameworks stretched at the seams.   

Many booming emerging markets from China to oil exporters in the Middle East have strict U.S. dollar exchange rate pegs.   

Yet, despite some growth rates in excess of 10 percent they are being forced to match Fed easing to keep those pegs -- flooding local banking systems with ever more cash as a result.   

The upward pressure on their exchange rates is intense and speculation they will have to break these links is rife.   

An example of the pressure on Hong Kong's peg, for example, was seen in data showing M3 money supply growth soaring to 28.3 percent year-on-year in September from 18.6 percent in August.   

Even if you believe the leading central banks are still in charge Thomas Mayer, chief European economist at Deutsche Bank, says the dilemma they face is that "there is no nice, middle way."   

Central banks have to choose between tolerating higher inflation in return for helping debtors honour nominal debts or forcing creditors into writeoffs that would see asset prices slump and recession ensue.   

As they seem to be choosing the former, markets are wary.   

Helping debtors repay is one thing, but if inflation erodes the value of those repayments, creditors will demand a price that will lead to higher borrowing costs.   

"If financial markets wake up to rising inflation -- then we have a problem," said Mayer.   
   RFGLOBAL-CENTRALBANKS (ANALYSIS)|LANGEN|ABX|BNX|FUN   NSe1202 : Interest Rates | nanl : Analysis | e12 : Economic/Monetary Policy | ecat : Economic News | ncat : Content Types   REusa : United States | namz : North America   PUBReuters Limited   ANDocument LBA0000020071031e3av001pc  



 HDThe Afternoon Report: Reckoning Rates      BYBy Matt Phillips      WC1,454 words   PD31 October 2007   SNThe Wall Street Journal Online   SCWSJO   LAEnglish   CYCopyright (c) 2007, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.   
   LPThe U.S. economy beefed up over the summer, despite weak-kneed financial and housing markets. Still, investors aren't expecting the data to keep the Federal Reserve from taking another whack at interest rates today.   
   TDIn its first pass at calculating growth for the July-through-September quarter, the Commerce Department estimated that gross domestic product -- a broad measure of the economy's physique -- rose at an annual rate of 3.9%, up a tick from the second quarter's 3.8% growth and besting the guesses of 24 Wall Street shamans surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires. Their consensus call was 3.2% GDP growth during the quarter. True enough, there was housing-related softness in the report, as residential investment tumbled 20% and zapped more than a percentage point of growth out of the final number. But there were plenty of off-setting bright spots, such as the undaunted millions piloting our national fleet of shopping carts. Consumer spending is the biggest component of GDP and it climbed 3% during the third quarter after posting more of a pipsqueak percentage, 1.4%, during the spring quarter. There were other signs of strength too, especially in exports, which shot up 16% thanks in part to the falling dollar, and business spending, which saw a 7.9% bump. In a separate report, U.S. construction spending unexpectedly jumped in September, with government-financed projects picking up the slack in the housing sector.   

Alas, nothing is simple for Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke and his elite squad of econo-wonks, who are, as of this writing, putting final flourishes on their latest masterpiece of a press release announcing their decision on interest rates. (It's due at 2:15 p.m.). The rosier-than-expected GDP and construction numbers might not have much bearing on the Fed's decision-making, which most handicappers expect will produce a quarter-percentage-point cut in the federal funds rate. That is mostly because these numbers offer a slightly stale view of the economy. "The question now is how well it can withstand even steeper declines in residential construction, the general tightening in credit conditions, falling house prices, oil prices at around $90 per barrel, and their negative implications for consumer and business spending," wrote Nigel Gault, an economist with research firm Global Insight. Such stresses would help make the case for easier credit.   

But not all dismal scientists discounted today's GDP figures. At Nomura Securities, analysts wrote that "the surprising GDP strength could be enough to persuade the FOMC to defer any decision about the need for another rate cut until a clearer picture of the fourth quarter has formed." And analysts with  Bear Stearns wrapped up their GDP commentary by noting refound strength in an employment report from payroll processing giant ADP. "With the evidence [from this morning's ADP report] suggesting that private payrolls grew around 100,000 in October, and with back-to-back quarters of near 4% real GDP growth, the economic argument for a further rate cut at this point is unclear to us." In any event, investors will be surprised with anything other than a quarter-point rate cut today, as they were pegging the chances for such a snip -- via fed-funds futures -- at more than 90% early in the day   

---   

Stocks Rise Ahead of Fed Decision   

Stocks advanced early Wednesday on encouraging third-quarter economic data ahead of the Federal Reserve's interest-rate decision this afternoon. The Dow Jones Industrial Average was up by 0.5% after midday. The S&P 500-stock index rose by about 0.8% and the Nasdaq Composite Index rose by more than 0.8%.  Google's shares passed $700 as news of the search giant's mobile-services plans continued to filter out. Bonds declined, with the 10-year note and the 30-year bond both sinking. Crude oil futures rose by more than $3 after midday. The dollar was mixed, losing ground against the euro and rising against the yen. European markets rose, and Asian markets were mixed.   

---   

Alcatel-Lucent to Speed Restructuring   

Struggling telecommunications-equipment maker  Alcatel-Lucent promised further job cuts and plans to shrink its top management team from 21 to seven as it contends with weak earnings since it completed its merger last December. Chief Executive Patricia Russo also announced that Chief Financial Officer Jean-Pascal Beaufret would be replaced. Analysts gave the announcement mixed reviews.   

---   

Euro-zone Inflation Picks Up   

Euro-zone inflation accelerated to a 25-month high in October and the unemployment picture brightened. But economic sentiment declined further, suggesting that the bloc's expansion will slow in the wake of the credit crunch, as well as indicating that the European Central Bank will resist raising rates for now.   

---   

China Raises Fuel Prices   

China will raise the prices of gasoline, diesel oil and aviation kerosene by 500 yuan ($67) per ton, or roughly 10%, starting from Nov. 1, as regional shortages of fuel spread and even reached Beijing. "The adjustment was made to shorten the gap between high-flying international crude prices and domestic oil prices," the National Development and Reform Commission said in announcing the move.   

Alleged Leader of Madrid Attacks Acquitted, Others Found Guilty   

A Spanish court convicted three lead defendants of murder in connection with the 2004 Madrid terror bombings, but acquitted an Egyptian who allegedly bragged that he masterminded the plot.   

---   

Deutsche Bank's Net Rises 31%; Capital Gains Offset Trading Losses   

Deutsche Bank posted slightly better-than-expected earnings as it was forced to take write-downs -- around 2.2 billion euros in the third quarter -- on assets that have proved harder to sell to investors after the credit markets turned sour this summer.   

MasterCard's third-quarter net income grew 63%, helped by a gain from an asset sale and continued strong growth in cardholder transactions, especially internationally. Shares jumped.   

Soaring commodity costs -- notably dairy -- and charges helped pull Kraft's net income down 20%.   

IAC/InterActiveCorp reported a 4.2% drop in third-quarter net income amid higher costs as home-shopping channel HSN showed signs of a turnaround.   

Pioneer and Hitachi posted weak earnings results for the fiscal second quarter and first half, respectively, as both companies struggled with weak sales of plasma TVs.   

Lazard's third-quarter net income tripled amid record results for the investment firm, including a strong merger-advisory performance.   

Newmont Mining, the world's second-largest gold producer by output, said third-quarter net income doubled, aided by surging gold prices, a tax gain and a joint-venture settlement.   

- - -   

Pakistan's Supreme Court Will Probe Bhutto Attack   

Pakistan's top judge said he will probe the bloody suicide attack on ex-Prime Minister Benazir Bhutto's homecoming parade, wading further into the country's deepening political turmoil. The blast during Ms. Bhutto's homecoming in Karachi on Oct. 18 killed more than 140 people and was the deadliest incident in a wave of violence that has raised fears for nuclear-armed Pakistan's stability. Also, aides say Ms. Bhutto is planning a trip outside of Pakistan to visit her family.   

---   

Bush to Name Agriculture Secretary   

President Bush is expected to nominate Edward Schafer, a former two-term Republican governor from North Dakota, to be his next secretary of agriculture today, according to a senior administration official. Mr. Schafer, who chose not to run again in North Dakota in 2000, will succeed Mike Johanns, who resigned as agriculture secretary last month to launch a bid for the Nebraska Senate seat being vacated by Republican Chuck Hagel at the end of next year.   

---   

Potential Bidding War Over Digital Map Maker   

Garmin, the biggest U.S. maker of car-navigation devices, unveiled an unsolicited $3.3 billion takeover offer for Dutch digital map maker Tele Atlas, topping a July offer by rival TomTom, of Amsterdam, by 15%.   

---   

Earthquake Rattles Bay Area   

The San Francisco Bay area's largest earthquake in nearly two decades rattled homes and nerves, but the magnitude-5.6 temblor caused no serious damage or injuries. Tuesday's night's tremor, centered about 9 miles northeast of San Jose, was the strongest to hit the Bay Area since October 1989, when the Loma Prieta earthquake caused 62 deaths and nearly $6 billion in damage.   

---   

Northern Rock Hires Blackstone as Adviser   

Troubled British mortgage lender  Northern Rock confirmed that it had hired  Blackstone Group as its third adviser in addition to  Merrill Lynch and  Citigroup as part of its strategic review, which it is due to complete by February.   

---   

The Associated Press contributed to this report.

 HDUPDATE 4-Fed cuts rates but signals scant appetite for more      BYBy Mark Felsenthal      WC798 words   PD31 October 2007   ET19:19   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Adds analyst's comment in paragraph 5, and primary dealer poll in paragraph 15)   

WASHINGTON, Oct 31 (Reuters) - The Federal Reserve cut U.S. interest rates by a quarter-percentage point on Wednesday to buffer the economy from a housing downturn, but suggested further rate reductions were far from a sure bet.   
   TDThe decision by the central bank's Federal Open Market Committee to lower the overnight federal funds rate to 4.5 percent -- a move that followed a more aggressive half-point cut last month -- was widely expected.   

But the Fed offered a bit of a surprise by saying the risk of inflation was about equal with downside risks to growth.   

That statement, coupled with a dissent from  Kansas City Federal Reserve Bank President Thomas Hoenig, who favored holding rates steady, threw cold water on expectations that additional reductions in borrowing costs are in store.   

"The Fed has no inclination to cut rates further unless the economic data suggest otherwise. Hence, barring another financial shock, the economic data will have to weaken for Fed easing to come back on the table," said Joseph LaVorgna, chief U.S. economist for  Deutsche Bank.

U.S. Treasury debt prices fell and stocks initially dropped as traders saw the Fed's statement suggesting a lower likelihood of further rate reductions.   

Stock prices, however, later turned around and the blue- chip Dow Jones industrial average <.DJI> closed up 137.54 points, while the dollar hit a record low against the euro.   

U.S. interest-rate futures contracts implied only a 42 percent chance the Fed will lower rates again at its next meeting in December, down from 64 percent overnight.   

In announcing its decision, the U.S. central bank said its actions should put the economy on better footing and help it weather the housing slump and related credit market woes.   

"The pace of economic expansion will likely slow in the near term, partly reflecting the intensification of the housing correction," the Fed said. But it said its rate cuts "should help forestall some of the adverse effects on the broader economy that might otherwise arise from the disruptions in financial markets and promote moderate growth over time."   

The decision by the Fed to lower the federal funds rate, which governs overnight borrowing between banks, led major banks to lower the prime rate they charge on loans to their best customers. It could also lead to lower rates on credit cards and auto loans for consumers; most mortgage rates, however, are tied to long-term rates set by the markets.   

TENSION IN THE RANKS   

In its statement, the central bank cautioned that it has not put to bed worries about price pressures, a point underscored by Hoenig's dissent.   

"Recent increases in energy and commodity prices, among other factors, may put renewed upward pressure on inflation," the Fed said. "In this context, the committee judges that some inflation risks remain.   

After this (rate) action, "the upside risks to inflation roughly balance the downside risks to growth," it added.   

A Reuters poll of 20 top bond trading firms that deal directly with the Fed in the markets found seven believe the rate-cutting cycle is now over. The median forecast from the firms was that rates would eventually bottom out one-quarter point lower at 4.25 percent.   

More details on the Fed's thinking are likely to emerge on Nov. 8 when Fed Chairman Ben Bernanke testifies before the congressional Joint Economic Committee.   

In addition to lowering the federal funds rate, the Fed's main economic lever, the central bank on Wednesday also trimmed the discount rate by a quarter-percentage point to 5 percent. The discount rate governs loans the Fed makes directly to banks having trouble finding the cash they need in the market.   

Credit markets, which were roiled in August as concerns mounted over rising delinquencies in the U.S. mortgage market, have regained some stability since the Fed lowered rates by a half-percentage point on Sept. 18.   

As policy-makers convened on Wednesday, a government report showed that growth in the third quarter was considerably stronger than most economists expected.   

U.S. gross domestic product, which measures total production within the country's borders, grew at an annual rate of 3.9 percent in the July-September period -- the strongest advance since the first quarter of 2006.   

A separate report showed private-sector employers added more jobs than expected in October, implying more underlying strength to the economy than previously thought. (Additional reporting by Glenn Somerville)

 HDUPDATE 4-Fed cuts rates but signals scant appetite for more      BYBy Mark Felsenthal      WC788 words   PD31 October 2007   ET22:47   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Adds analyst's comment in paragraph 5, and primary dealer poll in paragraph 15)   

WASHINGTON, Oct 31 (Reuters) - The Federal Reserve cut U.S. interest rates by a quarter-percentage point on Wednesday to buffer the economy from a housing downturn, but suggested further rate reductions were far from a sure bet.   
   TDThe decision by the central bank's Federal Open Market Committee to lower the overnight federal funds rate to 4.5 percent -- a move that followed a more aggressive half-point cut last month -- was widely expected.   

But the Fed offered a bit of a surprise by saying the risk of inflation was about equal with downside risks to growth.   

That statement, coupled with a dissent from  Kansas City Federal Reserve Bank President Thomas Hoenig, who favored holding rates steady, threw cold water on expectations that additional reductions in borrowing costs are in store.   

"The Fed has no inclination to cut rates further unless the economic data suggest otherwise. Hence, barring another financial shock, the economic data will have to weaken for Fed easing to come back on the table," said Joseph LaVorgna, chief U.S. economist for  Deutsche Bank.

 HDDollar lending stress persists after Fed rate cut      BYBy Natsuko Waki      WC499 words   PD1 November 2007   ET17:23   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LPLONDON, Nov 1 (Reuters) - Interbank dollar lending rates fell to 1-1/2 year lows on Thursday after the Federal Reserve cut key interest rates by a quarter point to 4.5 percent, but the Fed move failed to ease persistent distortions in the money market.   

The gap between the Fed's new target for overnight interbank funds and interbank dollar lending rates overseas -- a key gauge of the severity of the three-month old credit crunch by reflecting banks' hoarding of cash -- has crept higher.   
   TDAt 38 basis points, this spread is up 4 basis points from Wednesday and remains far above historical averages of 10-15 basis points.   

Spreads reached 70 basis points on August 10, when the credit crunch started. They were at 49 bps the day after the Fed cut interest rates by half a point in September.   

"Spreads have improved (in recent weeks) but they are still wide. The credit crisis in terms of interbank lending is not over. Banks are still hoarding liquidity and imposing higher borrowing costs," said Trevor Williams, head of group economic research at Lloyds Financial Markets.   

"However, absolute levels have come off and some people say that's what determines the economic activity. The cost of borrowing is not as bad as it appears to be on the surface in the interbank market."   

London interbank offered rate for three-month dollar deposits fell on Thursday to 4.8775 percent <LIBOR>, its lowest since March 2006. The rates for overnight dollar deposits fell to 4.64125 percent, also a 1-1/2 year low.   

Analysts say the future move in spreads depends on market expectations for future U.S. interest rate cuts.   

"We see little scope for the dollar Libor-funds spread to narrow below 30 bp and unless or until the market now turns to fully price in a further 25bp at the Dec 11th meeting, three month Libor could struggle to break below 4.80 percent in the near-term," Nick Parsons, head of market strategy at nabCapital, said in a note to clients.   

The sharp rise in interbank lending of August to September stemmed from the fallout in the U.S. subprime mortgage market and the near closure of the short-term asset-backed commercial paper (ABCP) market.   

Troubles in the credit market have prompted banks to hoard cash so that they could cover their potential losses in structured products exposed to the risky mortgage market.   

The ABCP market shows little sign of recovering, with Fed data showing the size of the U.S. ABCP market shrinking for a 12 straight week to $874.7 billion in the week ending October 31.   

Three-month euro rates fell to 4.59875 percent, their lowest since August 16 -- a day before the Federal Reserve delivered an emergency cut in the discount rate.

 HDMORTGAGES/AGENCIES-Prices lag Treasury rally as stocks slide   BYBy Julie Haviv      WC596 words   PD1 November 2007   ET20:46   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LPNEW YORK, Nov 1 (Reuters) - Prices of U.S. mortgage-backed securities and agency debt trailed sharply higher Treasuries on Thursday as a stock market sell-off and renewed credit market concerns caused investors to seek safety in government bonds.

The safe-haven buying failed to spill over into spread products issued by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac despite their triple-A ratings.
   TDThe rally in Treasuries offset losses garnered during the previous session after the Federal Reserve lowered interest rates and signaled it may refrain from further easing if the economy holds up.

"Investors are concerned about a diminished appetite for risk in the market," said Scott Kirby, sector leader for structured products at RiverSource Investments, an Ameriprise Financial Company based in Minneapolis.

"There are concerns about liquidation of positions and selling in the market due to further price erosion on troubled assets," he said. "In the near-term, I think price action in mortgage-backeds will be choppy and risk appetite will be pretty constrained."

The mortgage bond market over the near-term faces several obstacles, but Kirby said over the long-term the level of spreads in the market and the current level of compensation for taking risk is reasonably attractive at this point.

Kirby, who oversees roughly $25 billion in an array of different mortgage-type securities in RiverSource's roughly $100 billion fixed-income portfolio, has expectations that the appetite for risk will return and spreads will tighten and so investors will be rewarded for having risk on their books over time.

Active 15- and 30-year mortgage securities were 4/32 to 17/32 higher. Bond equivalent yields on 30-year 5.50 percent coupon MBS ranged from 5.563 percent to 5.760 percent.

Prices on 30-year 6 percent coupons were 8/32 to 9/32 higher. Bond equivalent yields ranged from 5.637 percent to 5.780 percent.

The yield premium on Fannie Mae MBS paying 6 percent interest versus the 10-year Treasury note widened to 1.368 percentage points on Thursday from 1.35 percentage points on Wednesday, according to Reuters data.

Fannie Mae 5.375 percent 10-year benchmark notes due June 12, 2017, were 2.5 basis points wider at 50.5 basis points bid and 48.5 basis points offered over Treasuries.

U.S. benchmark 10-year Treasury notes <US10YT=RR> were a full point higher at 103-5/32 to yield 4.348 percent. Yields, which move inversely to price, were down from Wednesday's 4.467 percent.

Government data showing tame core inflation and weaker consumer spending, together with a private-sector report of slower manufacturing growth, cut risk appetite and boosted bond prices. For more details, see [ID:nN01469383]

The stock sell-off and renewed credit worries led traders to put back bets that the Federal Reserve would lower rates again at its Dec. 11 policy meeting. U.S. interest rate futures implied roughly a 66 percent chance of another quarter-percentage point cut at the Fed's last 2007 meeting, up from 41 percent at Wednesday's close and roughly equal to the level prior the FOMC statement.

Market participants are now shifting their focus to data gauging the state of the labor market.

Friday's payroll report is typically one of the key market movers every month, primarily because the central bank's monetary policy is heavily reliant on economic data.

   LPLONDON, Nov 1 (Reuters) - Interbank lending rates for overnight and three-month dollar deposits fell to 1-1/2 year lows on Thursday, a day after the Federal Reserve cut interest rates by a quarter point.   

London interbank offered rate for three-month dollar deposits fell to 4.8775 percent at their daily fixing <LIBOR>, their lowest since March 2006, according to the data by the British Bankers Association.   
   TDThe rates for overnight dollar deposits fell to 4.64125 percent, also a 1-1/2 year low.

 HDUPDATE 1-CME Oct volume growth lower on Eurodollar slump      WC250 words   PD1 November 2007   ET16:54   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Adds details)   

CHICAGO, Nov 1 (Reuters) - CME Group , the world's largest derivatives exchange, said on Thursday that average daily trading volume in October dropped 18 percent from September, but was up 14 percent from a year ago.   
   TDThe year-on-year growth was the slowest for CME since it absorbed cross-town rival the Chicago Board of Trade in July. Total monthly volume was some 218 million contracts.   

Activity in CME's biggest contract, Eurodollar futures, was down 7 percent from a year ago to about 1.9 million contracts a day, and Eurodollar options volume dropped 15 percent from a year earlier.   

That helped pull year-on-year growth in the interest rate segment, CME's largest, to 5 percent.   

Eurodollar contracts are used to bet on or hedge against changes in Federal Reserve interest rate policy.   

By contrast, activity in e-mini stock index futures contracts expanded by 53 percent on the year and in foreign exchange contracts by 31 percent.   

CME said total electronic volume on its Globex trading platform averaged 7.7 million contracts a day, up 28 percent on the year.   

Electronic volume accounted for some 81 percent of monthly turnover, a new high. Turnover on CME's open-outcry trading floors was down 23 percent from a year ago, to an average of 1.7 million contracts a day.

   LPWASHINGTON, Oct 31 (Reuters) - The U.S. Federal Reserve resumed a meeting on Wednesday that analysts expect will deliver a quarter-percentage point cut in benchmark overnight interest rates to 4.5 percent.   

A Fed official said the meeting, which opened on Tuesday, resumed at 9 a.m. (1300 GMT). A statement outlining the Fed's interest-rate decision is expected around 2:15 p.m. (1815 GMT).   
   TDAnalysts expect the Fed to ease borrowing costs again as additional insurance for the U.S. economy against its slumping housing market and the fallout from a credit crunch.

 HDTaiwan dollar nearly flat U.S. on rate outlook, stocks      WC386 words   PD30 October 2007   ET02:53   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LP (Updates to close)   

 TAIPEI, Oct 30 (Reuters) - The Taiwan dollar closed nearly flat on Tuesday as the impact from falling stocks offset guarded anticipation of a U.S. interest rate cut this week.   
   TD Taiwan's currency <TWD=TP> closed at T$32.410 to the U.S. dollar compared with Monday's close of T$32.415 after reaching an intraday peak of T$32.335.   

 A U.S. interest rate cut would narrow the difference between U.S. and Taiwan rates, bolstering the local currency's appeal.   

 Uncertainty ahead of the Fed's decision held back the local currency, but few traders in Taiwan are worried, dealers said.  "Mainly that's because the Taiwan currency market isn't the same as markets internationally, as the central bank can step in," a dealer said. "So traders are optimistic."   

 The U.S. edged up from record lows against the euro and a basket of major currencies on Tuesday, but it remained under pressure from expectations that the Federal Reserve will lower interest rates when its two-day meeting ends on Wednesday and possibly again later this year.   

 The euro fell 0.1 percent to $1.4404 <EUR=>, off a record peak of $1.4439 hit on trading platform EBS on Monday, the highest since its launch in 1999.   

 The Fed is expected to cut rates by a quarter point to 4.5 percent on Wednesday to prevent the U.S. economy from sustaining broader damage from deterioration in the housing sector.   

 Taiwan stocks <.TWII>, which closed 0.53 lower on Tuesday after hitting a 7-year high a day earlier, also weighed on the local currency.   

 Oil importers were active on Tuesday, buying U.S. dollars and pressuring the Taiwan currency, although the impact was partially offset by exporters, who sought Taiwan dollars to meet their cash needs. The central bank also moved to keep the currency strong.   

 Volume on the  Taipei Forex Inc. was $1.282 billion at closing, less than the $1.704 billion at the same time on Monday.   

 On the smaller Cosmos exchange <COSMOS1> the Taiwan dollar closed at T$32.402, down from T$32.385 on Monday.

 HDRPT-Taiwan dollar strengthens on inflows, U.S. outlook      WC203 words   PD30 October 2007   ET03:11   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LP  (Repeats without change to text)   

 TAIPEI, Oct 30 (Reuters) - The Taiwan dollar strengthened again on Tuesday on foreign fund inflows and continued expectations of a U.S. interest rate cut this week.   
   TD Taiwan's currency <.TWD=TP> opened at T$32.345 to the U.S. dollar compared with Monday's close of T$32.415. It stood at T$32.395 as of 0208 GMT.   

 Dealers in Taipei said investors were trading cautiously ahead of a widely expected U.S. interest rate cut.  "Today we still have to look at what the United States is going to do," one dealer said. "The trend in Taiwan could reverse course."   

 Volume on the Taipei Forex Inc. was an active $97 million as of 0130 GMT, more than the $82 million at the same time on Monday.  The U.S. dollar had edged up on Tuesday after hitting a record low on Monday against a basket of major currencies, on expectations the U.S. Federal Reserve will cut interest rates this week and possibly again later this year.

 HDUS TREASURY OUTLOOK-Data drumbeat continues after rate cut      BYBy Ellen Freilich      WC745 words   PD31 October 2007   ET22:26   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LPNEW YORK, Oct 31 (Reuters) - The economic data drumbeat continues on Thursday after the Federal Reserve's latest interest-rate cut and with the Fed sounding flexible on future rate changes, truly fresh data will intrigue investors.   

The performance of the stock market is also likely to influence bond prices on Thursday, analysts said.   
   TDThe Fed cut U.S. interest rates on Wednesday, asserting that the risk of inflation was roughly equal to downside risks to growth. The statement that accompanied the rate cut suggested that further rate cuts were not a certainty and would be influenced by whether or not the economy could produce "moderate growth" on a path safe from recession.   

"The Fed's statement was designed to give the FOMC maximum flexibility, either to ease further, or take back recent steps, or keep policy unchanged," said  Daiwa Securities America chief economist Michael Moran.   

"It emphasized that the Fed is watching both the outlook for economic growth and inflation. It recognized the risk of the economy slowing down, but also talked about the risk that inflation could pick up," he added.   

The decision by the Fed's Federal Open Market Committee to cut the overnight federal funds rate by a quarter-percentage point to 4.5 percent was widely expected by the bond market.   

Fed officials had said that they expected the housing slump and the after-effects of tighter credit to weigh on the economy into next year. Slipping consumer confidence and tumbling home sales had also recently raised fears the economy might be weaker in coming quarters than the Fed had anticipated.   

One new clue to the economy's health will arrive with the Insitute for Supply Management (ISM) manufacturing index. Due at 10 a.m. EDT (1400 GMT) on Thursday, the ISM index will likely be the report of most interest to bond investors during the trading session because on Wednesday, the Chicago Purchasing Managers reported that its index (PMI) of regional manufacturing dipped below 50, a sign of contraction.   

Bond investors want to know if that slip below 50 foreshadowed a nationwide reduction in manufacturing during October. With housing weakening and credit conditions choppy, a downturn in manufacturing, which has been bolstered by the weak dollar, would create more concern about the economic outlook.   

Economists polled by Reuters offered a median estimate of 51.5 for the October ISM index, compared with 52.0 in September.   

JOBLESS CLAIMS, PERSONAL INCOME AND SPENDING   


 Two reports due earlier in the trading session -- jobless
claims and personal income and spending data -- should have
   

minimal impact on bond prices.   

The Labor Department's jobless claims numbers due early in the session are expected to show that new jobless claims remained fairly steady at 330,000 in the week ended Saturday, little changed from 331,000 new claims a week earlier.   

Investors have an eye on the labor market because a resilient job market supports consumer demand when it is under pressure from rising oil prices and weakening home values.   

But a much broader and more detailed view of the labor market will be revealed on Friday when the government releases its monthly employment data, this time for October.   

September personal income, spending, and inflation numbers, also due at 8:30 a.m. EDT, will offer more specific data on the state of the economy at the end of the third quarter. But these data ought to have little market impact since the third-quarter core personal consumption expenditure (PCE) price index, which included September, was released on Wednesday.   

STOCKS, STOCKS AND MORE STOCKS   

While bond investors absorb these economic data, they will also be minding the stock market, where the ebb and flow mirrors investors' varying appetite for risk. An increased tolerance for investment risk boosts stocks, but depletes demand for less risky U.S. government debt.   

If stock investors take some profits on Thursday, bond prices would benefit and their yields, which move in the opposite direction of prices, could ease. If stocks extend Wednesday's gains, bond prices could lose more ground.   

U.S. stocks rallied on Wednesday, buoyed in part by the Fed's interest-rate cut. Benchmark 10-year notes <US10YT=RR> traded 22/32 lower in price for a yield of 4.475 percent from 4.39 percent late on Tuesday.

 HDTREASURIES-Bonds leap in safety bid as stocks plummet      BYBy Chris Reese      WC593 words   PD1 November 2007   ET14:17   SNReuters News   SCLBA   LAEnglish   CY(c) 2007 Reuters Limited   
   LP(Updates prices)   

NEW YORK, Nov 1 (Reuters) - U.S. Treasuries prices leaped on Thursday as steep stock market losses and a resurgence in credit worries spurred demand for safe-haven investments a day after the Federal Reserve lowered interest rates.   
   TDGovernment data showing tame core inflation and weaker consumer spending, together with a private-sector report of slower manufacturing growth, also cut risk appetite and boosted expectations the Fed will lower rates at its December meeting. For details, see [ID:nN01406094]   

"It is all concerns about the implosions in the credit markets and that we are only seeing the beginning of it -- that it is going to hurt the economy and most likely force the Fed's hand again to ease," said Mary Ann Hurley, vice president of fixed income trading at  D.A. Davidson & Co. in Seattle.   

"Also the economic data today was really mixed at best for the economy," she said.   

U.S. benchmark 10-year Treasury notes <US10YT=RR> traded 30/32 higher in price for a yield of 4.36 percent from 4.47 percent late on Wednesday. Benchmark yields, which move inversely to prices, posted their biggest single-day dip in nearly eight weeks.   

"The risk-aversion trade is to be long Treasuries," said Brian Edmonds, head of rates trading with  Cantor Fitzgerald in New York.   

At the shorter end of the curve, two-year notes <US2YT=RR> traded 11/32 higher in price for a yield of 3.78 percent, from 3.95 percent. The two-year note took back more than Wednesday's price gains, when traders trimmed bets for future interest rate cuts after the Fed's policy statement raised doubts about tighter monetary policy.   

However, worries resurfaced in a big way on Thursday that further deterioration in the financial sector stemming from the housing slump and credit turmoil would hurt economic growth and pressure the Fed to lower rates in coming months.   

Short-term interest rate futures on Thursday afternoon implied a 64 percent chance the Fed will lower rates by 25 basis points again in December, to 4.25 percent, against 42 percent on Wednesday afternoon.   

The rate cut expectations were boosted after  Credit Suisse on Thursday reported a $1.9 billion write-down on bad loans and mortgage investments, and brokerage downgrades of  Citigroup raised concerns as to whether the biggest U.S. bank is under-capitalized.   

Citigroup shares ended down nearly 7 percent and the decline in financial stocks weighed on the broader equities market. The Dow Jones industrial average <.DJI> tumbled 2.6 percent. For the full market report, see [.N]   

Early in the session, data showing a slowdown in factory growth in October suggested the U.S. economy was cooling from its surprisingly robust third-quarter pace, even as data showed U.S. incomes edged higher in September while underlying consumer prices rose modestly.   

Data also showed the number of U.S. workers filing new claims for jobless aid fell more than expected last week, but the four-week moving average of claims edged up to a six-month high.   

Five-year notes <US5YT=RR> traded 21/32 higher for a yield of 4.03 percent from 4.18 percent, while the 30-year bond <US30YT=RR> traded 1-23/32 higher in price for a yield of 4.64 percent. (Additional reporting by Richard Leong)
